Chapter 03 · Physics

Electric Current

From the fundamental nature of charge to Ohm's Law, capacitors, and complete circuit analysis — this chapter covers all of electrostatics and basic circuit theory.

Electric Charge Coulomb's Law Electric Field Electric Potential Electric Flux Ohm's Law Resistance & Resistivity Capacitance
3.1

Electric Charge

Every atom contains electrons (negative), protons (positive), and neutrons (neutral). The property of particles due to which electric force exists between them is called electric charge.
9.1 × 10⁻³¹ kg
Mass of electron
1.6 × 10⁻²⁷ kg
Mass of proton/neutron
e = 1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹ C
Fundamental charge (electron/proton)
6.25 × 10¹⁸
Electrons in 1 Coulomb

Only electrons are transferred during charging. The substance that receives electrons becomes negatively charged; the one that loses electrons becomes positively charged. Every object is neutral by default.

n = 1/e = 1/(1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹) = 6.25 × 10¹⁸ electrons per coulomb
SI unit of charge: Coulomb (C)
Like charges repel. Unlike charges attract. Electric force is 10⁴³ times greater than gravitational force between two electrons!
3.2

Coulomb's Law

Coulomb's Law: The electric force between two stationary point charges is directly proportional to the product of their charges and inversely proportional to the square of the distance between them.
F = k · q₁q₂ / r²
k = 1/(4πε₀) = 8.9875 × 10⁹ ≈ 9 × 10⁹ N·m²·C⁻²
ε₀ = permittivity of free space = 8.854 × 10⁻¹² C²/Nm²
In CGS: k = 1 dyne·cm²/esu²
Force is along the line joining the two charges.
In a medium: Fₘ = (1/4πε) · q₁q₂/r²
F/Fₘ = ε/ε₀ = εᵣ = K (dielectric constant)
Relative permittivity εᵣ > 1 always, so Fₘ < F (force decreases in a medium)
When distance is doubled → force becomes 1/4th. When charges are doubled → force becomes 4 times.
Example: Total charge on 20 extra electrons
q = n × e = 20 × (−1.6 × 10⁻¹⁹) = −3.2 × 10⁻¹⁸ C
3.3

Electric Field, Potential and Flux

Electric Field (E)

The force per unit positive test charge placed at a point. It is the effect of a charge felt in the surrounding space.
E = F / q₀
E = kQ / r² = (1/4πε₀) · Q/r²
SI unit: N/C or V/m
Electric field is a VECTOR quantity.

Properties of Electric Field Lines

  1. Start from positive charges, end at negative charges
  2. Tangent at any point = direction of electric field at that point
  3. Two field lines NEVER intersect (would imply two directions at one point)
  4. Closer (denser) lines = stronger field; farther apart = weaker field
  5. Uniform field lines are parallel and equidistant
  6. Field lines are imaginary but electric field is real
  7. Field lines are always perpendicular to the conducting surface
  8. Field lines do NOT form closed paths

Electric Potential (V)

Work done against the electric field in bringing a unit positive charge from infinity to a point in the field.
V = W / q₀
VA = −kQ/r
Unit: Volt (V) = Joule/Coulomb
1 Volt = 1 J/C
Electric potential is a SCALAR quantity.
Potential Difference: VB − VA = −k·Q/rAB = −WAB/q₀

Electric Flux (φ)

The number of electric field lines passing perpendicularly through a given surface area.
φ = E · A · cos θ
E = electric field, A = area, θ = angle between E and normal to surface
SI unit: V·m or N·m²/C
Electric flux is a SCALAR.
If θ = 0°: φ = EA (maximum)  |  If θ = 90°: φ = 0 (parallel)
3.4

Ohm's Law, Resistance and Conductance

🗣
V = IR — Voltage = Current × Resistance. Series: Rtotal = R1+R2+... Parallel: 1/Rtotal = 1/R1 + 1/R2 + ... Power: P = VI = I²R = V²/R. Aa formulas directly numerical ma use thaay chhe!
Electric Current (I): The rate of flow of electric charge through a conductor.
I = Q/t
SI unit: Ampere (A). 1 A = 1 C/s
Current flows from + to − (conventional) but electrons flow − to +.

Ohm's Law

At constant temperature, the current through a conductor is directly proportional to the potential difference across it.
V = I × R  →  I = V/R  →  R = V/I
V = Voltage (Volts), I = Current (Amperes), R = Resistance (Ohms, Ω)
Ohm's Law is valid for metallic conductors at constant temperature. It does NOT apply to diodes, transistors, etc.

Resistance and Factors Affecting It

R = ρ × L / A
ρ (rho) = resistivity (specific resistance) of material (Ω·m)
L = length of conductor (m), A = cross-sectional area (m²)
More length → more resistance. More area → less resistance.
QuantitySymbolDefinitionUnit
ResistanceROpposition to current flowOhm (Ω)
ConductanceGG = 1/RSiemens (S) or mho (℧)
ResistivityρR per unit length per unit area: ρ = R·A/LΩ·m
Conductivityσσ = 1/ρS/m
Conductivity σ = 1/ρ = 1/(R·A/L) = L/(R·A)

Combination of Resistors

Series Combination

Rₛ = R₁ + R₂ + R₃ + … + Rₙ
Current same through all. Voltage divides.
Equivalent resistance is always GREATER than the largest individual resistance.

Parallel Combination

1/Rₚ = 1/R₁ + 1/R₂ + … + 1/Rₙ
Voltage same across all. Current divides.
Equivalent resistance is always LESS than the smallest individual resistance.
Series vs Parallel Resistors

Electrical Power

P = V × I = I²R = V²/R
Unit: Watt (W) = J/s. 1 kWh = 3.6×10⁶ J
3.5

Capacitance and Capacitors

Capacitance (C): The ability of a conductor to store electric charge. The ratio of charge stored to potential difference.
C = Q / V
SI unit: Farad (F) = Coulomb/Volt. Also written as °F.
1 μF = 10⁻⁶ F  |  1 nF = 10⁻⁹ F  |  1 pF = 10⁻¹² F

Parallel Plate Capacitor

Two parallel conducting plates of area A, separated by distance d, with a dielectric medium between them.

C = ε₀·A / d   (vacuum/air)
C = K·ε₀·A / d   (with dielectric)
ε₀ = 8.854 × 10⁻¹² F/m (absolute permittivity of free space)
K = εᵣ = dielectric constant (relative permittivity)
Capacitance ↑ if: A increases, d decreases, or K increases.
Condition for parallel plate capacitor: d² << A (plate separation much smaller than plate area).

Types of Capacitors

TypeConstruction
ElectrolyticThin metallic film (anode) + electrolyte paste (cathode) + oxide layer (dielectric)
MicaThin mica sheet sandwiched between conductor plates
PaperWax paper between tin plates
FilmPlastic film between thin conductive metal films
CeramicCeramic dielectric material

Series and Parallel Combinations of Capacitors

Series Combination

1/Cₛ = 1/C₁ + 1/C₂ + … + 1/Cₙ
Same charge Q on each capacitor. Voltage divides.
Equivalent C is SMALLER than smallest individual C.

Parallel Combination

Cₚ = C₁ + C₂ + … + Cₙ
Same voltage across each capacitor. Charge divides.
Equivalent C is LARGER than largest individual C.
Example: C₁=5μF, C₂=10μF, C₃=15μF
Series: 1/Cₛ = 1/5 + 1/10 + 1/15 = 6/30 + 3/30 + 2/30 = 11/30
Cₛ = 30/11 = 2.72 μF

Parallel: Cₚ = 5 + 10 + 15 = 30 μF
Chapter 3 — Key Formulas
Work, Energy ane Power nana lage chhe pan aa chapter na numericals tricky hoy chhe — formulas clear karo!
  • F = kq₁q₂/r²  |  k = 9×10⁹ Nm²C⁻²
  • E = F/q₀ = kQ/r²  |  φ = EA cosθ
  • V = kQ/r  |  C = Q/V
  • V = IR (Ohm's Law)  |  R = ρL/A
  • Series R: Rₛ = R₁+R₂+…  |  Parallel: 1/Rₚ = 1/R₁+1/R₂+…
  • Series C: 1/Cₛ = 1/C₁+1/C₂+…  |  Parallel: Cₚ = C₁+C₂+…
  • C = ε₀A/d  |  P = VI = I²R = V²/R
🎯 Chapter 3 — Exam Tips (Electric Current)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: Ohm's Law ane Series/Parallel — aa thi direct MCQ pakka aave chhe!
1
Ohm's Law: V = IR. Resistance increases with temperature for metals (positive temperature coefficient). For semiconductors: resistance DECREASES with temperature.
2
Series circuit: Same current, voltage divides. R_total = R₁+R₂+... | Parallel circuit: Same voltage, current divides. 1/R_total = 1/R₁+1/R₂+...
3
Power: P = VI = I²R = V²/R. Electric energy = Pt (joules) = VIt. Commercial unit: kWh. 1 kWh = 1 unit of electricity = 3.6×10⁶ J.
4
Coulomb's Law: F = kq₁q₂/r². If distance doubles, force becomes ¼. k = 9×10⁹ Nm²C⁻². Charge of electron = −1.6×10⁻¹⁹ C.
5
Capacitor: Stores charge, blocks DC, passes AC. C = ε₀A/d (parallel plate). Series: smaller equivalent C. Parallel: larger equivalent C (opposite of resistance!).
6
Fuse wire = low melting point alloy (tin + lead). MCB = Miniature Circuit Breaker (modern fuse). Earthing prevents electric shock. Three-pin plug: longest pin = earth.

Chapter 04 · Physics

Heat and Thermometry

Understanding heat as energy transfer, temperature scales and their conversions, how heat travels through materials, and the thermal properties of substances.

Heat & Temperature Temperature Scales Celsius ↔ Fahrenheit ↔ Kelvin Modes of Heat Transfer Heat Capacity Specific Heat Thermal Conductivity Linear Thermal Expansion
4.1

Heat, Temperature and Units

Heat

  • A form of energy
  • Transferred between bodies due to temperature difference
  • Flows from higher T to lower T
  • SI unit: Joule (J)
  • CGS unit: erg
  • Practical unit: calorie (cal)

Temperature

  • Degree of hotness or coldness
  • Determines direction of heat flow
  • One of 7 fundamental quantities
  • SI unit: Kelvin (K)
  • Practical: °C, °F
1 calorie = heat needed to raise 1 g of water from 14.5°C to 15.5°C
1 calorie = 4.186 joule (Joule's mechanical equivalent)
W/Q = J (constant), where W = work, Q = heat, J = 4.186 J/cal
4.2

Temperature Scales and Conversion

🌡️ Temperature Scales — Visual Comparison
Celsius °C
100°C
Water Boils

Water Freezes
-273°C
Absolute Zero
°F = (9/5)×°C + 32
K = °C + 273
°C = (°F − 32) × 5/9
Kelvin K

Water Boils

🗣
Simple trick: K = °C + 273. Etle 25°C = 298 K. Body temp 37°C = 310 K. Aa conversions numericals ma direct kaam aave chhe!
ScaleFreezing point of waterBoiling point of waterDivisionsInventor
Celsius (°C)0 °C100 °C100 equal partsCelsius, 1710
Fahrenheit (°F)32 °F212 °F180 equal partsFahrenheit, 1717
Kelvin (K) — Absolute273.15 K (273 K)373.15 K (373 K)Same as CelsiusLord Kelvin
Temperature Scale Comparison

Conversion Formulas

(tC − 0)/100 = (tF − 32)/180 = (tK − 273)/100
°C to °F: tF = (9/5)·tC + 32
°F to °C: tC = (5/9)·(tF − 32)
°C to K: tK = tC + 273.15
K to °C: tC = tK − 273.15
Fever of 101°F — Convert to °C and K
tC = (5/9)(101 − 32) = (5/9)(69) = 38.33 °C
tK = 38.33 + 273 = 311.33 K
At what temperature do Celsius and Fahrenheit coincide?
Let θ = tF = tC
θ/5 = (θ − 32)/9 → 9θ = 5θ − 160 → 4θ = −160 → θ = −40°
Both scales read −40 at the same temperature.
Absolute zero in Fahrenheit
tF = (9/5)(−273.15) + 32 = −491.67 + 32 = −459.67 °F ≈ −460 °F
Absolute zero (0 K = −273.15°C): The lowest possible temperature. At 0 K, the pressure of an ideal gas would become zero. Below this temperature, gas volume would be negative — impossible.
4.3

Modes of Heat Transfer

ModeMedium needed?How it worksExamples
ConductionYes (solid)Heat passes through material without movement of matterIron rod heated at one end, cooking utensil
ConvectionYes (fluid: liquid/gas)Heat transferred by actual movement of heated particlesBoiling water, sea breeze, room heating
RadiationNo (works in vacuum)Electromagnetic waves (infrared) transfer energy without mediumSun's heat reaching Earth, feeling warmth near fire

Convection — Natural vs Forced

Natural convection: Driven by gravity. Hot particles rise (less dense), cold particles sink. Examples: sea breeze, trade winds, heating water in a flask.

Forced convection: A pump or fan forces fluid movement. Examples: car radiator cooling system, forced-air home heating, human blood circulation (heart as pump).

Radiation Properties

  • Does NOT need a medium — travels through vacuum
  • Travels at speed of light (3 × 10⁸ m/s)
  • Heat radiation = electromagnetic waves, wavelength range: 8×10⁻⁷ m to 4×10⁻⁴ m (infrared region)
  • When radiation falls on a body: partly reflected, partly absorbed, partly transmitted
  • Dark/black bodies absorb and emit more radiant energy than light-colored bodies
  • Factors affecting radiant energy emitted: temperature, nature of surface, area of surface
4.4

Heat Capacity and Specific Heat

Heat Capacity (Hc)

The ratio of heat given to a body to the corresponding change in its temperature.
Hc = Q / ΔT
SI unit: J/K  |  Practical: cal/°C or cal/K

Heat capacity depends on both the material AND mass of the body.

Specific Heat (c)

The quantity of heat required per unit mass to raise the temperature of a body by 1 unit. It depends only on the material, NOT the mass.
c = ΔQ / (m × ΔT)  →  ΔQ = m·c·ΔT
SI unit: J/(kg·K)  |  CGS: cal/(g·°C)
specific heat = Heat capacity / mass
MaterialSpecific Heat (J/kg·K)Specific Heat (cal/g·K)
Water (H₂O)41901.0
Sea water39000.93
Ice (−10°C)22200.530
Aluminium (Al)9000.2150
Copper (Cu)3860.0923
Silver (Ag)2360.0564
Water has the highest specific heat among common substances — it can absorb or release large amounts of heat with little temperature change. This is why oceans moderate Earth's climate.
4.5

Thermal Conductivity

Thermal Conductivity (K): The intrinsic ability of a solid to conduct heat through it. Different substances have different thermal conductivities.

Good conductors: copper, silver, metals. Bad conductors (insulators): glass, wood, rubber, air.

Formula for Heat Conduction (Steady State)

ΔQ/Δt = K · A · (T₁ − T₂) / d
ΔQ/Δt = rate of heat flow (W)
K = coefficient of thermal conductivity
A = cross-sectional area (m²)
T₁ − T₂ = temperature difference (°C or K)
d = thickness / distance between faces (m)
SI unit of K: W·m⁻¹·K⁻¹ (= J·s⁻¹·m⁻¹·K⁻¹)
CGS unit: cal·s⁻¹·cm⁻¹·°C⁻¹
Temperature Gradient: The rate of fall of temperature per unit distance in the direction of heat flow = ΔT/Δx.

Steady State: When temperature of every cross-section becomes constant and there is no more heat absorption. Only thermal conductivity matters (not specific heat) in steady state.
Example 1: Copper rod
Copper rod: length 19 cm = 0.19 m, area = 0.785 cm² = 785×10⁻⁷ m², T₁=100°C, T₂=30°C, t=10 min=600 s, K=380 W/mK
Q = KA(T₁−T₂)t/d = 380 × 785×10⁻⁷ × 70 × 600 / 0.19
Q = 380 × 785 × 70 × 600 × 10⁻⁷ / 0.19 = 6594 J ≈ 6.59 kJ
Example 2: Glass window
A = 0.5 m², d = 6 mm = 6×10⁻³ m, T inside = 23°C, T outside = 2°C, K = 1.0 W/mK, t = 1 hr = 3600 s
Q = 1.0 × 0.5 × (23−2) × 3600 / (6×10⁻³)
Q = 0.5 × 21 × 3600 / 0.006 = 37800/0.006 = 6,300,000 J = 63 × 10⁵ J

Applications of Thermal Conductivity

ApplicationPrinciple
Cold storage rooms: thick walls of brickBrick = bad conductor → reduces heat flow from outside
Cooking utensil handles made of rubber/eboniteBad conductor → protects hands from heat
Ice wrapped in sawdustAir in sawdust pores = insulator → prevents melting
Two bedsheets better than one thick sheetAir layer between sheets = bad conductor → better insulation
Eskimo igloos (double-walled)Trapped air between walls prevents heat loss
Metallic handle feels colder than wooden doorMetal conducts more body heat away → feels colder
Glass cracks on rapid coolingGlass = poor conductor → uneven cooling creates stress → cracks

Linear Thermal Expansion

When a solid is heated, its length increases. The fractional change in length per degree Celsius rise in temperature is the coefficient of linear expansion (α).
L = L₀(1 + α·ΔT)
ΔL = L₀ · α · ΔT
L₀ = original length, L = new length, ΔT = temperature change
α = coefficient of linear expansion (per °C or per K)
Unit of α: °C⁻¹ or K⁻¹
Area Expansion: A = A₀(1 + β·ΔT) where β = 2α
Volume Expansion: V = V₀(1 + γ·ΔT) where γ = 3α
β = coefficient of superficial (area) expansion = 2α
γ = coefficient of cubical (volume) expansion = 3α
Relationship: α : β : γ = 1 : 2 : 3
Bimetallic strips bend when heated because two metals have different coefficients of linear expansion — one expands more, causing the strip to curve. Used in thermostats and bimetallic thermometers.
Chapter 4 — Key Points
Light na laws ane mirror/lens formula DDCET ma pakka aave j chhe — sign convention bhulsho nahi!
  • °C to °F: tF = (9/5)tC + 32  |  °F to °C: tC = (5/9)(tF−32)
  • K = °C + 273.15 (use 273 for calculations)
  • °C and °F coincide at −40°  |  °F and K coincide at 574.6
  • 1 calorie = 4.186 J  |  Q = mcΔT
  • Rate of conduction: ΔQ/Δt = KA(ΔT)/d
  • K unit: W m⁻¹ K⁻¹  |  ΔL = L₀αΔT
  • Radiation travels at 3×10⁸ m/s through vacuum
🎯 Chapter 4 — Exam Tips (Heat & Thermometry)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: Temperature conversion ane heat transfer modes — quick MCQ aave chhe!
1
Temperature conversion: °C to °F: (°C × 9/5) + 32. °F to °C: (°F − 32) × 5/9. K = °C + 273. TRICK: −40°C = −40°F (only point where both scales are equal!).
2
3 modes of heat transfer: Conduction (solid-to-solid, needs medium) | Convection (fluids — liquids & gases, needs medium) | Radiation (no medium needed, travels at speed of light).
3
Specific heat of water = 4200 J/kg·K (HIGHEST among common substances). This is why water is used as coolant. Sea water moderates coastal climate.
4
Q = mcΔT (heat formula). m=mass, c=specific heat, ΔT=temp change. Ice melting: Q = mL (latent heat). Latent heat of ice = 336,000 J/kg.
5
Thermal expansion: Solids expand least, gases expand most. Linear expansion: ΔL = L₀αΔT. Railway tracks have gaps for expansion. Overhead wires sag in summer.
6
Newton's Law of Cooling: Rate of cooling ∝ temperature difference with surroundings. Black body absorbs AND emits maximum radiation. Shiny surfaces reflect, absorb less.

Ch5 = Wave, Optics & Acoustics — Refraction, Snell's law, TIR, optical fiber, LASER, echo. Snell's law: n₁sinθ₁ = n₂sinθ₂. Echo = sound wall thi reflect thaay chhe, minimum 17m distance joie!
Chapter 05 · Physics

Wave Motion, Optics and Acoustics

From the fundamental nature of waves to the magic of total internal reflection, laser technology, fiber optics, and the science of sound in rooms — complete coverage for DDCET 2026.

Types of Waves Wave Equations Refraction & Snell's Law Refractive Index Total Internal Reflection Critical Angle Optical Fiber LASER Sound & Echo Reverberation & Sabine
5.1

Types of Waves

Wave: A disturbance that transfers energy from one point to another without transferring matter.
ClassificationTypeDescriptionExamples
By medium requirementMechanical wavesRequire a material medium to propagateSound waves, water waves, seismic waves
Non-mechanical (EM waves)Do NOT require a medium — travel through vacuumLight, X-rays, radio waves, gamma rays
By particle motion vs wave propagationTransverse wavesParticle vibration ⊥ (perpendicular to) wave propagation directionLight waves, water surface waves
Longitudinal wavesParticle vibration ∥ (parallel to) wave propagation direction — compressions and rarefactionsSound waves, P-waves (earthquakes)
By wave formProgressive (Traveling) wavesEnergy and waveform travel forward continuouslyLight from a source, sound traveling in open air
Stationary (Standing) wavesWave pattern doesn't travel — nodes (zero amplitude) and antinodes (max amplitude) fixed in spaceGuitar string vibrating, sound in a closed pipe

Progressive Wave vs Stationary Wave — Detailed Comparison

FeatureProgressive (Travelling) WaveStationary (Standing) Wave
Energy transferEnergy travels forward continuouslyNo net energy transfer — energy is stored
AmplitudeSame for all particles (at same instant)Varies — zero at nodes, maximum at antinodes
WaveformMoves forward with wave velocityDoes not move — fixed in space
NodesNo nodes — all particles vibrateNodes (zero displacement) at fixed positions
PhaseAdjacent particles have different phasesAll particles between 2 nodes are in same phase
FormationSingle wave from sourceSuperposition of two identical waves traveling in opposite directions
ExampleSound in open air, EM waves in spaceVibrating string (fixed both ends), sound in pipes
Transverse Wave vs Longitudinal Wave
5.2

Wave Parameters and Equations

ParameterSymbolDefinitionUnit
AmplitudeAMaximum displacement of a particle from its rest/equilibrium positionm
Wavelengthλ (lambda)Distance between two consecutive points in the same phase (e.g., crest to crest)m
Time PeriodTTime for one complete oscillation/cycles
Frequencyf or nNumber of complete cycles per secondHz (s⁻¹)
Wave SpeedvDistance traveled by wave per unit timem/s
PhaseφThe position/state of the wave at a given instantradians
IntensityIEnergy transmitted per unit time per unit areaW/m²
Angular frequencyωω = 2πfrad/s
Wave numberkk = 2π/λrad/m
v = f × λ = λ/T
Wave speed = frequency × wavelength
Wave equation: y = A sin(ωt − kx)
y = displacement at position x and time t
A = amplitude, ω = 2πf = angular frequency, k = 2π/λ = wave number
Intensity ∝ A² (Intensity proportional to square of amplitude)
I = P/Area (for a point source: I ∝ 1/r²)

Electromagnetic Wave Spectrum

TypeFrequency (Hz)WavelengthKey Property/Use
Radio waves10⁴ – 10⁹km – cmBroadcasting, communication
Microwaves10⁹ – 10¹²cm – mmRadar, microwave ovens, Wi-Fi
Infrared (heat)10¹¹ – 4×10¹⁴0.7 μm – 1 mmThermal imaging, remote controls
Visible light4×10¹⁴ – 7×10¹⁴400–700 nmVision, photography
Ultraviolet7×10¹⁴ – 10¹⁷10–400 nmSterilization, sunburn
X-rays10¹⁷ – 10²⁰0.01–10 nmMedical imaging
Gamma rays>10²⁰<0.01 nmCancer treatment, nuclear reactions
All EM waves travel at speed of light c = 3 × 10⁸ m/s in vacuum.

Sound Waves

Sound is a longitudinal, mechanical wave. It needs a medium — cannot travel through vacuum.

TypeFrequency RangeUse
InfrasonicBelow 20 HzNatural disasters sensing, elephant communication
Audible20 Hz – 20,000 Hz (20 kHz)Human hearing range
UltrasonicAbove 20,000 HzMedical sonography, sonar, SONAR, industrial cleaning, bat navigation

Speed of sound in air at 0°C ≈ 332 m/s; at room temperature ≈ 343 m/s.

5.3

Refraction, Refractive Index and Snell's Law

Refraction: The bending of light when it passes from one medium to another. Occurs because the speed of light is different in different media.

Laws of Reflection (Important Prerequisite)

Reflection: Bouncing back of light (or any wave) from a surface.
  1. The angle of incidence (i) = angle of reflection (r). Both measured from the normal to the surface at the point of incidence.
  2. The incident ray, reflected ray, and normal at the point of incidence all lie in the same plane.
Laws of reflection apply to ALL types of mirrors (plane, concave, convex) and to all waves (light, sound, water waves). Total Internal Reflection also obeys these same laws of reflection.

Optically rarer medium (like air): higher speed of light. Optically denser medium (like glass): lower speed of light.

Laws of Refraction

  1. The incident ray, refracted ray, and normal at the point of incidence — all lie in the same plane.
  2. Incident ray and refracted ray are on opposite sides of the normal.
  3. Going rarer→denser: bends TOWARDS normal (angle of refraction < angle of incidence: r < i)
  4. Going denser→rarer: bends AWAY from normal (r > i)
  5. A ray hitting normal (i=0°) passes straight through without bending.
  6. Snell's Law: sin i / sin r = constant = refractive index

Refractive Index and Snell's Law

μ = η = sin i / sin r = constant (Snell's Law)
Relative refractive index of medium 2 w.r.t. medium 1: μ₂₁ = sin i / sin r = v₁/v₂
v₁, v₂ = speeds of light in medium 1 and medium 2
Symbol: μ (mu) or η (eta) or n
MediumAbsolute Refractive Index
Vacuum / Air1.0 (reference)
Water1.33
Ice1.31
Glass (crown)1.52
Glass (flint)1.62
Diamond2.42
Diamond has the highest refractive index among common substances. This is why it sparkles — total internal reflection occurs easily due to small critical angle (24°).

Effects of Refraction in Daily Life

  • A pencil placed in water appears bent/broken at the surface
  • Objects at the bottom of water appear shallower than they are
  • Difficult to catch a fish — its position appears different from actual
  • Lateral shift: when light passes through a glass slab, emergent ray is shifted parallel to incident ray
5.4

Total Internal Reflection (TIR) and Critical Angle

Total Internal Reflection (TIR): When light travels from a denser medium to a rarer medium, and the angle of incidence exceeds the critical angle, the light is completely reflected back into the denser medium — none passes through.

Critical Angle (θc)

The angle of incidence (in the denser medium) for which the angle of refraction becomes exactly 90° (refracted ray travels along the boundary).
sin θc = nᵣ / nᵢ = n₂/n₁
θc = sin⁻¹(nᵣ/nᵢ)
nᵢ = refractive index of denser (incident) medium
nᵣ = refractive index of rarer medium
For water-air: θc = sin⁻¹(1/1.33) ≈ 48.6°
For crown glass-air: θc = sin⁻¹(1/1.52) ≈ 41.14°
For crown glass-water: θc = sin⁻¹(1.33/1.52) ≈ 61.0°
For diamond-air: θc = sin⁻¹(1/2.42) ≈ 24°
Derivation: nᵢ sin θc = nᵣ sin 90° = nᵣ (since sin 90° = 1)
∴ sin θc = nᵣ/nᵢ

Necessary Conditions for TIR

  1. Light must travel from denser medium → rarer medium
  2. Angle of incidence must be greater than the critical angle (i > θc)
TIR does NOT occur when light goes from rarer to denser medium. Higher refractive index → smaller critical angle → easier TIR.
Total Internal Reflection — What Happens as Angle Increases

Applications of TIR

ApplicationExplanation
Sparkling of diamondsCritical angle = 24° (very small). Most light undergoes TIR inside diamond and exits only from top → brilliant sparkle
Mirage in desertHot air near ground has low density (rarer) — light from sky bends and undergoes TIR → appears like water
Looming (polar regions)TIR in cold dense air layers → ships appear to float in air
Optical fiberLight undergoes repeated TIR inside the fiber core and travels long distances
Endoscope (medical)Uses TIR in optical fibers to see inside the body
5.5

Optical Fiber

Optical Fiber: A thin, flexible strand of high-quality glass, quartz, or plastic that transmits light (and information) over long distances using Total Internal Reflection.

Construction

PartDescription
CoreCentral part — high refractive index (n₁). Light travels through here. Diameter: ~8-10 μm (single mode) or 50-200 μm (multimode)
CladdingLayer surrounding core — lower refractive index (n₂). Ensures TIR at core-cladding boundary. Condition: n₁ > n₂
Outer jacketProtective plastic coating
Condition for light propagation: n₁ > n₂ (core denser than cladding)
Numerical Aperture (NA) = √(n₁² − n₂²)
NA indicates the light-gathering capacity of the fiber. Higher NA = more light accepted.

How Light Travels in Optical Fiber

  1. Light enters one end of the fiber at the acceptance angle
  2. Hits core-cladding boundary at an angle greater than critical angle
  3. Undergoes TIR — bounces along the fiber
  4. Even if fiber is curved or twisted, TIR continues
  5. Light emerges from the other end
Even if angle of incidence exceeds the acceptance angle at entry, light will NOT propagate in the fiber.

Types of Optical Fibers

TypeDescription
Single-mode step-index fiberCore diameter ~8-10 μm. One path for light. Used in long-distance communication. Most widely used.
Multimode step-index fiberCore diameter ~50-200 μm. Multiple paths. More signal loss.
Multimode graded-index fiberRefractive index decreases gradually from center. Reduces dispersion.

Applications of Optical Fiber

  • Telecommunications: Internet cables, phone lines — carry signals as light pulses at high speed. Optical fibers carry far more information than copper wires (wider bandwidth).
  • Medical (Endoscopy): Flexible fiber bundles allow doctors to view inside the body without surgery. Used in colonoscopy, bronchoscopy, etc.
  • Military and sensing: Secure communication, fiber optic sensors
  • Broadcasting: Cable TV, data transmission

Estimated lifespan of optical fiber: 20–30 years. Phenomenon responsible for signal loss: scattering and absorption.

Light sources used in fiber optic communication: LEDs (Light Emitting Diodes), Semiconductor lasers, and Phototransistors (used as detectors at the receiving end).

5.6

LASER

LASER = Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation
2020 marked the 60th anniversary of the invention of the LASER (first laser demonstrated in 1960 by Theodore Maiman).

Characteristics of Laser Light

#PropertyExplanation
1MonochromaticSingle wavelength (single frequency/colour)
2CoherentAll photons are in the same phase (or constant phase difference)
3Directional (Unidirectional)Travels in one straight direction with very little divergence — beam cross-section barely changes over long distances
4High Intensity & BrightnessEnergy concentrated in a narrow beam — extremely bright
5Parallel raysRays in laser beam are parallel to each other
6Stimulated emissionProduced by stimulated (induced) emission — NOT spontaneous emission

Laser vs Ordinary Light

Ordinary Light (Bulb)

  • Spreads in ALL directions (divergent)
  • Intensity decreases rapidly with distance
  • Polychromatic (many wavelengths)
  • Incoherent (photons in different phases)
  • Low intensity
  • Cannot be focused to a very tiny spot

LASER

  • Travels in ONE direction (non-divergent)
  • Intensity remains almost constant
  • Monochromatic (single wavelength)
  • Coherent (all photons in phase)
  • Very high intensity
  • Can be focused to microscopic spot

Applications of LASER

  • Metal cutting and hole drilling (industrial)
  • Welding (precise, clean)
  • Eye surgery (LASIK), cancer treatment
  • Barcode scanners in shops
  • CD/DVD read/write laser
  • Optical mouse (computer)
  • Fiber optic communication (laser as light source)
  • Surveying, remote sensing, LIDAR
  • Testing physical and chemical properties of materials
5.7

Echo, Reverberation and Sabine's Formula

Echo

Echo: A repetition of sound heard after reflection from a distant hard surface. The original sound and its echo are heard as separate sounds.
Condition for echo: Minimum distance from reflecting surface = v×t/2
For distinct echo: time gap ≥ 1/10 s (0.1 s — minimum persistence of hearing)
At 343 m/s (speed of sound at room temperature):
Min. distance = 343 × 0.1/2 ≈ 17 m

Reverberation

Reverberation: The persistence of sound in an enclosed space after the source has stopped. It occurs due to multiple reflections of sound from walls, ceiling, floor, etc.

In echo — the reflected sound arrives AFTER the original has ended (>0.1 s gap).
In reverberation — reflected sounds overlap with the original, prolonging it.

Reverberation Time (RT or T): The time taken for sound intensity to fall to one millionth (10⁻⁶) of its original value after the source stops. Equivalently, time for sound level to fall by 60 dB.
Optimal reverberation time for speech: 0.5–1.0 s. For music: 1.5–2.5 s. Too much reverberation → sounds blend, unclear speech. Too little → sounds dead, flat.

Sabine's Formula

T = 0.161 × V / A
T = reverberation time (seconds)
V = volume of the room (m³)
A = total absorption in the room (sabins) = Σ(aᵢ × Sᵢ)
aᵢ = absorption coefficient of ith surface material
Sᵢ = area of ith surface (m²)
Absorption coefficient (a) = Sound energy absorbed / Sound energy incident
Ranges from 0 (perfect reflection) to 1 (complete absorption)
Unit of total absorption A: sabin (1 sabin = 1 m² of perfectly absorbing surface)
Understanding Sabine's Formula
A lecture hall has volume V = 3000 m³ and total absorption A = 200 sabins.
T = 0.161 × 3000 / 200 = 483/200 = 2.415 seconds
(This is too high for a lecture hall — would need more absorbing material to reduce it to ~1 s)

Absorption Coefficient Values

MaterialAbsorption Coefficient (approx.)
Open window / door1.0 (perfect absorber — reference)
Carpet0.2 – 0.5
Curtains (heavy)0.5 – 0.7
Brick wall0.02 – 0.05
Concrete0.01 – 0.02
Audience (seated)0.4 – 0.5
Wooden floor0.05 – 0.1

Acoustic Design of Auditoriums

  • Walls covered with sound-absorbing materials (felt, carpets, perforated boards)
  • Curved reflecting surfaces direct sound to all areas
  • No sharp echoes from back wall — use sound absorbers
  • Volume chosen carefully for required reverberation time
  • Audience itself contributes to absorption

  • v = fλ  |  T = 1/f  |  ω = 2πf  |  y = A sin(ωt − kx)
  • Snell's Law: sin i / sin r = μ = constant
  • μ = v₁/v₂ (ratio of speeds) = n (refractive index)
  • Critical angle: sin θc = n₂/n₁ (rarer/denser refractive index)
  • TIR occurs when: light goes denser→rarer AND i > θc
  • Diamond θc = 24°, Glass θc ≈ 41°, Water θc ≈ 49°
  • Optical fiber: n₁(core) > n₂(cladding) for TIR; NA = √(n₁²−n₂²)

🎯 Chapter 5 — Exam Tips (Wave, Optics & Acoustics)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: TIR, Snell's law ane echo — aa 3 topics thi pakka MCQ aave chhe!
1
Snell's Law: n₁sinθ₁ = n₂sinθ₂. Also: n = sin i / sin r = v₁/v₂ (speed ratio). Refractive index > 1 means light slows down in that medium.
2
TIR conditions (BOTH must be true): (1) Light must go from DENSER to RARER medium AND (2) angle of incidence > critical angle (θc). NOT possible rarer→denser!
3
Critical angles: Diamond θc = 24° (highest n = 2.42), Glass θc ≈ 41° (n = 1.5), Water θc ≈ 49° (n = 1.33). Higher refractive index → SMALLER critical angle.
4
Optical fiber: Core (denser) surrounded by cladding (rarer). TIR keeps light inside. Applications: Medical endoscopes, internet data cables, decorative lamps.
5
LASER: Light Amplification by Stimulated Emission of Radiation. Properties: Monochromatic (one wavelength), Coherent (same phase), Collimated (parallel rays). Uses: Surgery, CD/DVD, barcode readers.
6
Echo: Min. distance = 17 m (sound at 343 m/s, ear needs ≥0.1s gap). Reverberation = multiple echoes. Sabine formula: T = 0.161V/A. Acoustics: concert halls need T = 1.5–2.5 s.

🗣
Ch1 = Ecosystem — Ecosystem components, food chain, food web, pyramid of numbers. 10% energy transfer rule PAKKO yaad karo. Producer → Herbivore → Carnivore → Top predator — aa order badalasho nahi!
1

Ecosystem

Classification · Components · Food Chain · Biotic & Abiotic
Weightage: ~10%
What is an Ecosystem?
🗣
"Eco" = Greek ma "oikos" = home. Ecosystem = ek jagah jya biotic (jivit) ane abiotic (nir-jiv) vastu interact kare chhe. Sun energy input aape chhe, decomposers energy/nutrients return kare chhe — circular process!

An ecosystem is a community of living organisms (plants, animals, microbes) interacting with each other and with their non-living (physical) environment as a system. Earth itself is the largest ecosystem.

The word "eco" comes from Greek oikos meaning "home." An ecosystem is essentially a home where living and non-living things interact constantly. Energy flows and matter cycles within it.

Eco = Greek ma "home" — ecosystem matlab saad bhashama: jivit ane nir-jiv vastu na gharna aapas ma interaction!
Classification of Ecosystem

Ecosystems are broadly divided into Natural and Artificial ecosystems.

// Ecosystem Classification Tree
ECOSYSTEM
↙                 ↘
🌍 Natural Ecosystem
🏗️ Artificial Ecosystem
↙    ↘
🌲 Terrestrial
💧 Aquatic
↓
🌾 Agriculture
🐠 Aquarium
🏙️ Urban
🏭 Industrial

🌲 Terrestrial (Land)

  • Forest ecosystem
  • Grassland ecosystem
  • Desert ecosystem (Babool, Cactus plants)

💧 Aquatic (Water)

  • Marine – sea, ocean
  • Fresh water
    • Lotic (Running): River, Stream
    • Lentic (Standing): Lake, Pond
  • Estuarine – where river meets sea

Fish house / Aquarium is the most common example of an artificial ecosystem asked in DDCET MCQs. The Sea is the largest ecosystem on Earth.

Lotic vs Lentic: Lotic = flowing/running water (river, stream). Lentic = still/standing water (lake, pond). Easy trick: Lotic = Lots of movement.

Components of Ecosystem

Every ecosystem has two types of components:

🏗️ (A) Structural Components

  • Biotic – living things (plants, animals, microbes)
  • Abiotic – non-living things (air, water, soil, sunlight, temperature)

⚙️ (B) Functional Components

  • Food chain
  • Food web
  • Bio-Geo Chemical Cycles (Carbon cycle, Nitrogen cycle, Water cycle)
Biotic Components (Living Things)

Biotic = all living organisms in an ecosystem. Classified into 3 groups based on their role:

// Biotic Component Classification
BIOTIC COMPONENTS
↙            ↓            ↘
🌿 Producers
Green plants, Algae, Bacteria
🐄 Consumers
Herbivores, Carnivores, Omnivores
🍄 Decomposers
Bacteria, Fungi, Earthworms, Termites
1. Producers (Autotrophs)

Organisms that can make their own food. They are the building blocks of every ecosystem.

Producers

  • Green plants & Algae → use sunlight to convert CO₂ + water → glucose + O₂ (Photosynthesis)
  • Some bacteria → use energy from inorganic chemicals to make food (Chemosynthesis)
Photosynthesis: CO₂ + H₂O + Sunlight → Glucose + O₂

The most important element in the composition of biotic components is Carbon (C). Organisms contain about 49% carbon. In DNA and RNA, the important element is Phosphorous (P).

2. Consumers (Heterotrophs)

Organisms that cannot make their own food — they get energy by eating other organisms. Also called heterotrophs.

Type Also Called Feeds On Examples
Herbivores Primary / 1st order consumers Plants only Cow, deer, goat, grasshopper
Carnivores Secondary / 2nd order consumers Animals (herbivores) Fox, snake, birds eating insects, hawks
Tertiary consumers 3rd order consumers Secondary consumers Lion, tiger
Omnivores Opportunistic feeders / top-level carnivores Plants AND animals Humans, frog

In a food web, Humans are both primary and secondary consumers (omnivores). They eat plants (primary) and animals (secondary). Answer = (c).

3. Decomposers (Reducers / Saprotrophs)

Microorganisms that break down dead organic material of producers and consumers into simpler substances. They recycle nutrients back into the soil.

Decomposers

  • Bacteria – attack animal tissues
  • Fungi – attack plant tissues
  • Earthworms, Termites

Also called "reducers" because they reduce the volume of organic material. Also called saprotrophs.

4. Scavengers (Detritivores)

Animals that feed on dead bodies of other organisms. Example: Earthworms, termites. Also called saprotrophs.

Abiotic Components

Non-living physical and chemical factors in the environment:

🌡️ Temperature
💧 Water
🌬️ Air
🪨 Soil / Minerals
☀️ Sunlight
🌧️ Rainfall
Food Chain & Food Web
🌱 Food Chain — Flow Diagram
🌿 Plants
Producers (Autotrophs)
Photosynthesis thi food banaave
→ Energy →
🐛 Herbivores
Primary Consumers
Plants khaay chhe
→ Energy →
🦊 Carnivores
Secondary Consumers
Herbivores khaay chhe
→ Energy →
🦁 Top Predator
Tertiary Consumer
Food chain no end
🍄 Decomposers — Dead matter ne decompose kare chhe (Bacteria, Fungi)
🗣
Energy flow: Sun → Plants → Herbivores → Carnivores → Top Predator → Decomposers. Energy 10% j transfer thaay chhe — baaki 90% heat thaay chhe! Aa 10% law DDCET ma pakko aave chhe!
Food Chain

A food chain is a linear sequence showing how energy and nutrients pass from one organism to another through eating.

🌿 Producer
Grass
→
🐛 Herbivore
Grasshopper
→
🐸 Carnivore
Frog
→
🐍 Carnivore
Snake
→
🦅 Top Consumer
Eagle

In a food chain, energy flows from trophic level-1 (producers) to higher levels (1→4). Energy is LOST at each step (~90% lost, only 10% passed on). This is the 10% law.

Food Web

A food web is a complex network of multiple interconnected food chains. It gives a more realistic picture of feeding relationships in an ecosystem.

Pyramid of Numbers

A pyramid of numbers shows the number of organisms at each trophic level. At the base (producers), population is maximum. As you go up, numbers decrease.

In pyramid of numbers, Producers have maximum population. This is because energy decreases at every level, so fewer organisms can be supported at higher levels.

Composition of Atmosphere (Key Facts for MCQs)

Major Gases in Air

  • Nitrogen (N₂) → 78.08%
  • Oxygen (O₂) → 20.95%
  • Argon (Ar) → 0.93%
  • CO₂ → 0.03%

Environment Spheres

  • Hydrosphere – sea, rivers, lakes
  • Atmosphere – air around Earth
  • Lithosphere – land/crust
  • Biosphere – zone of life

The component of environment made of sea, rivers, lakes = Hydrosphere. Maximum pollutants are found in the Hydrosphere.

Mangrove forestsNear sea shore on marshy land

IUCN (International Union for Conservation of Nature) is located in Switzerland.

Drug causing death of vultures = Diclofenac (veterinary drug).

At present, about 1.75 Earths are required to sustain present human population.

🎯 Chapter 1 — Exam Tips (Ecosystem)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: Ecosystem na basic terms thi MCQ bahu aave chhe — producers, consumers, decomposers pakka karo!
1
Largest ecosystem = Ocean/Sea (not forest). Artificial ecosystem = Aquarium, Farmland, Garden. Natural = Forest, Desert, Pond.
2
Producers = Autotrophs (make own food via photosynthesis). Consumers = Heterotrophs (depend on others). Decomposers = Bacteria & Fungi (break down dead matter).
3
Food chain: Producer → Primary consumer → Secondary → Tertiary. ENERGY FLOWS ONE WAY — cannot be recycled. Only 10% energy transferred to next level (10% rule).
4
Atmosphere composition: N₂ = 78%, O₂ = 21%, Ar = 0.93%, CO₂ = 0.04%. N₂ is the MOST abundant gas in atmosphere.
5
Biotic = living components (plants, animals, microbes). Abiotic = non-living (water, air, soil, temperature, light). Both are part of ecosystem.
2

Pollution & Its Types

Air · Water · Noise · Solid Waste · Plastic · E-Waste
Key Topic
What is Environmental Pollution?

Environmental pollution = any undesirable change in physical, chemical, or biological characteristics of any component of environment (air, water, land) that causes harmful effects on various life forms or property.

Pollution = harmful substances environment ma mix thay. Air, Water, Soil, Noise — chare types DDCET ma important! Sources: industrial, agricultural, domestic. Effects ane control measures pakka yaad karo!

Pollution dates back to when humans first discovered fire. Burning fossil fuels (coal, oil, gas) releases poisonous gases.

🌋 Natural Causes

  • Volcanic eruptions
  • Earthquakes
  • Storms and dust
  • UV radiations
  • Decay of organic matter (releases H₂S)

🏭 Man-made Causes

  • Commercial & industrial activities
  • Over population, Urbanisation
  • Insecticides, pesticides
  • Use of vehicles, Mining
  • Nuclear explosions
  • Construction of buildings, roads, dams
Types of Environmental Pollution
💧 Water pollution
💨 Air pollution
🏜️ Land pollution
🔊 Noise pollution
☢️ Radioactive pollution
🌡️ Thermal pollution
Pollutant

A pollutant = any substance present in environment in concentration that alters quality of environment and affects living things adversely. NOT all pollutants are harmful if present in small amounts (e.g., nitrogen, phosphorus, sulphur in small amounts increase soil fertility).

Classification of Pollutants

♻️ Biodegradable Pollutants

Degraded naturally by bacteria. Examples:

  • Municipal waste water (sewage)
  • Wood, Paper, Garbage, Cardboard

⛔ Non-biodegradable Pollutants

Cannot be broken down naturally. Examples:

  • DDT, Plastics, Aluminium
  • Heavy metals like lead, mercury

MCQ shortcut: Paper is biodegradable. DDT, Plastic, Aluminium are NOT biodegradable.

Air Pollution
🗣
Air pollution na major sources: Vehicles (CO, NOx), Industries (SO₂), Burning (particulate matter). CO = colorless, odorless, deadly! CO₂ na vadhara thi greenhouse effect. CFC = ozone deplete kare chhe — refrigerators ma vaparay!

Definition (IS:4167-1966): "Air pollution is the presence in ambient atmosphere of substances, generally resulting from human activity, in sufficient concentration and for sufficient time that causes harmful effects on humans, plants and animals."

Scales of Air Pollution

Indoor (Micro scale)

Inside buildings, homes. E.g., cooking smoke.

Outdoor / Ambient (Meso scale)

Local outdoor area. E.g., city smog.

Global (Macro scale)

Global level. E.g., ozone depletion, greenhouse effect.

Sources of Air Pollution

🌿 Natural Sources

  • Atmospheric chemical reactions
  • Volcanic eruptions (gases + ash)
  • Forest fires (smoke + greenhouse gases)
  • Dust storms, electric storms
  • Marsh gases (decaying vegetation)
  • Pollen (from trees, grasses, weeds)
  • Salt spray from oceans
  • Microorganisms (algae, fungi, bacteria)
  • Radioactive substances (uranium, radium, thorium)

🏭 Man-made Sources

  • Combustion of fuel – coal, wood, oil, LPG
  • Automobile exhaust – CO (carbon monoxide)
  • Industries – iron/steel, refineries, chemical plants
  • Thermal power plants – SO₂
  • Nuclear power plants – radioactive fly ash
  • Agricultural activities – pesticides
  • Construction materials – bricks, cement dust
  • Sanitation system – garbage, foul gases
  • Mining, Nuclear explosions, Aircraft
  • Waste water treatment plants

CFC (Chlorofluorocarbon) – produced from refrigerators, air conditioners, and paints. Contains Carbon, Hydrogen, Chlorine and Fluorine. Major cause of ozone layer depletion.

Effects of Air Pollution on Human Health
PollutantEffect
CO + NO with haemoglobinForms carboxyhaemoglobin → reduces oxygen-carrying capacity of blood
NOₓ and SOₓIrritation to eyes, throat, nose; asthma, bronchitis
PAN (photochemical smog)Irritation of eyes, nose, throat, respiratory diseases
Aromatic hydrocarbonsCancer
Dust, smog, sootAsthma, bronchitis, lung cancer
Silica-containing dustSilicosis
LeadNervous system damage, kidney damage, vision problems
Mercury (fossil fuel combustion)Nerve, brain, kidney damage
Radioactive substancesLung diseases, cancer

Minamata disease is caused by Mercury poisoning. Bhopal Gas Tragedy (Dec 1984) was caused by Methyl Isocyanate (MIC) leaked from Union Carbide company.

Effects on Plants
  • Air pollutants enter through stomata (leaf pores), destroy chlorophyll, affect photosynthesis
  • Pollutants erode the waxy coating of leaves called cuticle → leaf drop
  • Particulates (dust, fog, soot) block stomata → reduce transpiration rate
  • SO₂ affects marble, limestone, roofing, paper, textiles, monuments
Effects on Climate

CO₂ and greenhouse gases → temperature rises → melting of ice caps and glaciers → rise in sea level. About 50% of greenhouse effect is attributed to CO₂.

Effects on Animals
  • Lead poisoning near smelters → paralysis, difficulty breathing, loss of appetite
  • Arsenic poisoning → salivation, vomiting, abnormal temperature, death
  • Fluorine toxicity in cattle and sheep → lack of appetite, diarrhoea, muscle weakness, death
Automobile Exhaust Contains
Hydrocarbons
Carbon Monoxide (CO)
Lead vapour
SO₂
Photochemical Smog

Formed when NOₓ + Hydrocarbons + Sunlight react in atmosphere → forms PAN (Peroxyacetyl Nitrate).

NOₓ + Hydrocarbons + Sunlight → PAN (Photochemical Smog)

Main product of photochemical smog = PAN. Gas harmful to Taj Mahal = SO₂ (sulphur dioxide). Air pollutant that reduces oxygen-carrying capacity of blood = CO (Carbon Monoxide).

Control of Air Pollution
  1. Use LPG/CNG instead of diesel/petrol in automobiles
  2. Grow more trees (trees absorb atmospheric CO₂)
  3. Do not burn plastic wastes
  4. Use bicycles; use public transport
  5. Process changes in industries (e.g., wash coal before pulverisation)
  6. Control dust in cement plants (rotary kiln)
  7. Fix catalytic converters in vehicles
  8. Reduce use of pesticides and chemical fertilisers
  9. Use electrostatic precipitators in thermal power plants
  10. Use controlling equipment: cyclone separator, fabric filter, wet scrubber
Air Pollution Control Equipment
1. Gravitational settling chamber
2. Cyclone separator
3. Electrostatic precipitators
4. Fabric filters
5. Wet scrubbers

In a cyclone separator, the force used to remove particulate matter from gas is centrifugal force.

Water Pollution

Any physical, biological, or chemical change in water quality that adversely affects living organisms or makes water unsuitable for use = water pollution.

Signs of Polluted Water
  • Bad taste or odour
  • Offensive odours from rivers, lakes, oceans
  • Reduction in number of fish / aquatic life
  • Oil or grease floating on surface
  • Unchecked growth of aquatic weeds
  • Presence of colour due to organic matter
Major Sources of Water Pollution
  • Sewage (discharge of sewers and drains)
  • Industrial effluents (chemical, dye, paper, tannery)
  • Intensive use of chemical fertilisers
  • Use of insecticides in fields
  • Synthetic detergents
  • Oil spillage during drilling and shipment
  • Discharge from nuclear power plants / research centres
  • Radioactive ash from nuclear explosions
  • Dead animals thrown in water bodies
  • Waste heat from industrial discharge and thermal plants

BOD = Biochemical Oxygen Demand – measures the amount of organic (carbonic) matter in domestic sewage. Higher BOD = more polluted water. pH of acidic water = less than 7.

Noise Pollution

🔊 Sound vs Noise

SoundNoise
What we hearUnpleasant, unwanted sound
Constant pitch (frequency)Variable pitch
Periodic motionNon-periodic motion
Meaningful communicationNo meaningful communication
Unit: Hertz (Hz)Unit: Decibel (dB)

📊 Measurement

Intensity of sound = sound energy received per second per unit area.

dB = 10 log₁₀ (I / I₀)

Where I₀ = 10⁻¹² W/m² (reference intensity)

Acceptable noise in urban residential area = 35–45 dBA

Repeated sound of 150 dB reduces hearing capacity.

Effects of Noise Pollution

Physical Effects

  • Loss of hearing (>80–90 dBA for 8+ hrs/day)
  • Total deafness (acoustic trauma)
  • Damage to ear drum from sudden loud sounds

Physiological Effects

  • Headache, nausea
  • Dizziness, gastric ulcers
  • High heart beat rate
  • Blood pressure and sugar fluctuations

Psychological Effects

  • Annoyance, insomnia (sleep interference)
  • Depression, fatigue
  • Mental stress, speech interference
  • Decrease in efficiency
Control of Noise Pollution
  1. Control at receiver's end – ear plugs, ear muffs, noise helmets, headphones
  2. Suppression at source – soundproof chambers, proper maintenance, sound-absorbing materials, silencers, quieter machines, vibration damping (rubber, cork, neoprene under machines)
  3. Acoustic zoning – silence zones near hospitals, schools, residential areas; keep industrial areas, bus terminals, railway stations, aerodromes away from residential areas
  4. Planting trees – green trees along roadsides reduce noise
  5. Sound insulation – acoustical tiles, perforated plywood in recording rooms
  6. Legislative measures – Noise Pollution Act, restrict loud speakers near silence zones
Solid Waste Management (SWM)

Solid waste = all solid and semi-solid materials discarded by a community. Arises from human and animal activities.

Types of Solid Waste

1. Refuse

General term for all rejected/worthless solid wastes. Includes garbage + rubbish + ashes + industrial wastes. Does NOT include body waste (excreta).

2. Garbage

Putrescible organic waste from kitchens, hotels, restaurants (vegetable/fruit peelings, waste food). Density: 450–900 kg/m³. Decomposes quickly.

3. Rubbish

Non-putrescible wastes excluding ashes. Paper, rags, glass/plastic bottles, broken crockery, furniture. Density: 50–400 kg/m³.

4. Ashes

Incombustible waste from houses, industries, furnaces, chulhas. Density: 700–850 kg/m³.

More Definitions

5. Putrefaction

Anaerobic decomposition of organic matter by anaerobic/facultative bacteria in absence of oxygen.

6. Leachate

Liquid that has travelled through solid waste and has dissolved materials. Non-biodegradable dumped waste + rain water → coloured liquid = leachate.

Municipal Solid Waste (MSW)

Waste from domestic and commercial activities. Also called "refuse." Includes garbage, rubbish, ashes, dust, construction wastes, dead animals.

India MSW: ~65 million tons/year
Projected by 2031: ~125 million tons
Land required: 1250 hectares/year
Objectives of SWM
  1. Remove discarded materials from inhabited places in timely manner
  2. Prevent spread of disease
  3. Minimise likelihood of fires
  4. Reduce aesthetic insults from putrifying organic matter
  5. Dispose refuse minimising hazards to environment
Three Basic Functional Elements of SWM
1. Collection
2. Disposal
3. Recycling/Reutilisation
Plastic Waste & Its Hazards

Plastics = synthetic organic polymers. Used in everything from water bottles to medical supplies. Despite initial assumptions of being harmless, plastic disposal has caused major environmental problems.

Global production 2018: 380 million tonnes
Since 1950: 6.3 billion tonnes produced
Only 9% recycled, 12% incinerated
Yearly in oceans: 13 million tonnes
Marine lives killed: ~100,000/year
Toxic Constituents in Plastics
Phthalates
Poly-fluorinated chemicals
Bisphenol A (BPA)
Brominated flame retardants
Antimony trioxide
Environmental Effects of Plastic Waste
  • Environmental natural beauty deterioration
  • Entanglement and death of aquatic organisms (260+ marine species)
  • Sewage system blockage → mosquito breeding
  • Reduction in water percolation and soil aeration → reduced agricultural productivity
  • Chlorinated plastics leach toxic chemicals into soil → contaminate groundwater
  • Methane (dangerous greenhouse gas) released during biodegradation of plastics
  • Plastics in oceans release polystyrene and BPA → water pollution
  • Open burning → dioxins, PCBs, furans → respiratory disorders
  • Microplastics – bioaccumulate in food chain

BPA (Bisphenol A) is used in inner linings of food cans, reusable water bottles, and baby bottles. Long-term exposure leaches BPA into food/drinks. In women: polycystic ovarian syndrome, obesity, recurrent miscarriages.

E-Waste (Electronic Waste)

E-waste = electrical and electronic equipment that is discarded by consumers or bulk consumers, or rejects from manufacturing, refurbishment and repair processes.

E-waste Generation in India

India's Position

  • 4th largest e-waste producer in the world
  • Computer devices: 70% of e-waste
  • Telecom sector: 12%
  • Medical equipment: 8%
  • Electrical equipment: 7%
  • Individual household: 15%

Statistics

  • Global: 20–50 million tons disposed/year
  • = 5% of all Municipal Solid Waste
  • Annual growth rate: 10%
  • 10 states contribute 70% of total e-waste
  • 65 cities generate 60% of total e-waste
  • Top city: Mumbai, then Delhi, Bengaluru, Chennai, Kolkata, Ahmedabad
  • Top state: Maharashtra, then Tamil Nadu, UP
Constituent Materials of E-waste
Iron & Steel: 50%
Plastics: 21%
Mercury, Arsenic, Lead: 13%
  • Useful metals: iron, steel, copper, aluminium
  • Precious metals: platinum, gold, silver
  • Hazardous materials: mercury, lithium, barium, radioactive isotopes
  • Toxic substances: Dioxins, PCBs, Lead, Zinc, Cadmium

Maximum material found in e-waste = Ferrous metal (Iron & Steel = 50%). E-Waste (Management) Rules 2016 were published by India's Ministry of Environment, Forest and Climate Change [Amended 2018].

Effects on Human Health
  • Electronic gadgets contain lead, cadmium, chromium, mercury, PVC, brominated flame retardants, beryllium, antimony, phthalates
  • Long-term exposure damages: nervous systems, kidneys, bones, reproductive and endocrine systems. Some are carcinogenic and neurotoxic.
  • Children are especially vulnerable
Effects on Environment
  • E-waste landfilled → contaminated leachates → pollutes groundwater
  • Acids/sludge from melting chips → acidification of soil → water contamination
  • Toxic heavy metals follow "soil-crop-food pathway" → heavy metal exposure to humans
  • Incineration → toxic fumes/gases → air pollution
  • Open air burning of circuit boards (plastic) to recover copper → air pollution
  • Burning computer monitors → dioxins → cancer
Recycling of E-waste (Important)
ComponentHow Recycled
PlasticSent to recyclers → fence posts, plastic sleepers, trays, vineyard stakes
MetalScrap sent to recyclers → new steel and metallic materials
Glass (from CRTs)Retrieved from Cathode Ray Tubes → complex process (hazardous materials)
MercurySent to specialized mercury recycling facilities
Printed Circuit BoardsSmelted → recover silver, tin, gold, palladium, copper
Hard DrivesShredded → aluminium ingots for automotive industry
Ink/Toner CartridgesReturned to manufacturer → remanufactured or separated into metal/plastic
BatteriesHulled for plastic → metals smelted → recover nickel, steel, cadmium, cobalt

🎯 Chapter 2 — Exam Tips (Pollution)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: Pollution ke 4 types ane dareek na sources + effects yaad karo — MCQ direct aave chhe!
1
4 types: Air | Water | Soil (Land) | Noise. Air pollution = primary (direct emission) vs secondary (formed in atmosphere). Smog = smoke + fog.
2
Acid rain = pH < 5.6. Caused by SO₂ and NO₂ reacting with water. Damages buildings (marble), forests, fish. Taj Mahal = acid rain victim.
3
Water pollutants: BOD (Biochemical Oxygen Demand) — higher BOD = more polluted. Eutrophication = excess nutrients → algae bloom → O₂ depletion.
4
E-Waste: Contains toxic metals — Lead, Mercury, Cadmium, Arsenic. Never burn e-waste. Should be sent to certified recyclers. Growing problem due to electronics.
5
Noise pollution: >85 dB causes hearing damage. Sources: traffic, industries, loudspeakers. Decibel (dB) is logarithmic scale. Normal conversation = 60 dB.
3

Climate Change

Greenhouse Effect · Global Warming · Ozone Layer Depletion
Key Topic
What is Climate Change?

Climate change refers to any significant change in climatic factors such as temperature, wind, precipitation, lasting for an unexpected period.

🌡️ Global Warming

Refers specifically to the rise in temperature of Earth.

🌍 Climate Change

Refers to all changes in climate = global warming + changes in rainfall, wind, sea level, etc.

Climate change ≠ Global warming. Global warming is one part of climate change. Climate change = global warming + all other changes.

Causes of Climate Change
  1. Change in the sun's intensity
  2. Slow changes in Earth's orbit around the sun
  3. Change in ocean circulation
  4. Human activities (main cause today):
    • Burning fossil fuels
    • Deforestation
    • Desertification
    • Urbanisation
    • Industrialisation
Effects of Climate Change

Direct Effects

  • Extreme hot and cold spells
  • Wet or dry spells of rainfall
  • Cyclones, Floods
  • Rise in sea level
  • Global warming

Cascading Effects

  • Change in rainfall pattern
  • Serious water shortage
  • Change in crop pattern
  • Growth of microorganisms → spread of diseases
  • Melting of polar ice
  • Adverse effects on ecosystem
Greenhouse Effect & Global Warming

The global average temperature has increased by about 1°C in the last century. The main cause is the abnormal increase in concentration of greenhouse gases in the atmosphere.

🌿 How Greenhouse Effect Works

Think of a car parked in sunlight. Sunlight enters through glass windows (like atmosphere), heats the interior, but the heat cannot escape — the glass traps it. Similarly, greenhouse gases trap heat radiated by Earth.

☀️ Sunlight hits Earth
→
Earth radiates heat
→
GHGs trap heat
→
🌡️ Temperature rises
Main Greenhouse Gases (GHGs)
#GasFormula% contribution
1Carbon dioxideCO₂~50% of greenhouse effect
2MethaneCH₄Significant contributor
3Nitrous OxideN₂O–
4ChlorofluorocarbonCFCAlso depletes ozone
5OzoneO₃At troposphere level
6Water vapourH₂ONatural contributor

Gas mainly responsible for greenhouse effect = CFC (in MCQ context — though CO₂ contributes most in reality, CFC is the answer for the exam as per book).
O₂ is NOT a greenhouse gas. Methane (CH₄) and CO₂ are main GHGs.
About 50% of greenhouse effect = CO₂.

Steps to Control Global Warming
  1. Reduction in use of fossil fuels for energy generation
  2. Promote non-conventional energy: wind, solar, nuclear
  3. Hydrogen as future energy source
  4. Reduce N₂O emission by minimising nitrogen fertilisers in agriculture
  5. Increase vegetation cover / forests (forests are "sinks" for CO₂ absorption)

Reason for sea level rise = Global warming (melting of glaciers and polar ice).

Ozone Layer

The ozone layer is located in the stratosphere, about 15 to 30 km above Earth's surface. About 90% of atmospheric ozone is concentrated here.

The ozone layer absorbs most of the sun's ultraviolet (UV) radiation and protects life on Earth. It acts like a natural sunscreen — also called the ozone shield or Earth's protective umbrella.

The ozone layer absorbs = Ultraviolet rays. NOT x-rays, infrared rays, or G-rays. Mainly responsible for ozone layer depletion = CFC (Chlorofluorocarbon). CFC used in refrigerators contains Carbon, Hydrogen, Chlorine and Fluorine.

Effects of Ozone Depletion
  • More UV-B radiation (200–280 nm) reaches Earth's surface
  • UV-B affects DNA and photosynthetic chemicals → mutation and cancer
  • Cases of skin cancer (disfigurement) will increase
  • Easy absorption of UV by eye lens and cornea → increase in cataract
  • Destruction of melanin-producing cells → immunosuppression; fair-skinned people at greater risk
  • Decrease in phytoplankton population → affects zooplankton, fish, marine animals, whole aquatic food chain
  • Decrease in yield of crops: corn, rice, soyabean, cotton, bean, pea, wheat
  • Degradation of paints, plastics, polymer materials → economic loss
International Agreements on Climate Change
Paris Agreement (2015)
Montreal Protocol
Earth Summit
Kyoto Protocol
Vienna Convention
Copenhagen Conference
Doha Climate Conference (2012)

🎯 Chapter 3 — Exam Tips (Climate Change)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: Greenhouse gases ane Ozone layer — DDCET ma pakka aave chhe!
1
Greenhouse gases: CO₂ (main), CH₄ (methane), N₂O, CFCs, Water vapour. CO₂ is primarily responsible for global warming — burning fossil fuels releases it.
2
Greenhouse effect: Atmosphere traps heat like glass in greenhouse. Natural GE = essential for life (keeps Earth warm). Enhanced GE = problem (too much warming).
3
Ozone layer: At 15–35 km altitude (stratosphere). Absorbs harmful UV radiation. Destroyed by CFCs (chlorofluorocarbons used in ACs, fridges). Montreal Protocol (1987) banned CFCs.
4
Ozone hole = largest over Antarctica. UV causes: skin cancer, cataracts, immune suppression, crop damage. Ozone depletion is measured in Dobson Units (DU).
5
Global warming effects: Polar ice melting → sea level rise → floods. Changing weather patterns. Coral bleaching. Species extinction. Reduce by: renewable energy, reducing deforestation.
4

Renewable Sources of Energy

Wind · Solar · Biomass · Tidal · Geothermal · Hydro · Hydrogen
Very Important
Introduction to Renewable Energy

Renewable energy = energy from sources that are naturally replenished on a human timescale (sun, wind, water, geothermal heat, biomass). Unlike fossil fuels, they don't run out.

Renewable = nature dobara banave (inexhaustible). Non-renewable = limited (coal, petrol). Solar, Wind, Hydro, Tidal, Geothermal, Biomass, Hydrogen — aa 7 renewable sources DDCET ma aave chhe! Advantages + limitations dareek na yaad karo!

✅ Renewable Sources

  • Wind energy
  • Solar energy
  • Biomass / Biogas
  • Tidal energy
  • Geothermal energy
  • Hydro energy
  • Hydrogen energy
  • Forests and vegetation

❌ Non-Renewable Sources

  • Petroleum
  • Coal
  • Natural gas
  • Fossil fuels
  • Nuclear energy (uranium, plutonium)

For sustainable development, Renewable energy is recommended. By using conventional (fossil fuel) energy → it produces pollution. Solar energy is the only continuously available renewable source.

⚡ Wind Energy

Wind = air in motion, caused by uneven heating of Earth's surface by the sun. The kinetic energy of moving air is proportional to wind speed.

Wind energy is used to run turbines to generate electricity. It is cheap and pollution-free.

Min wind speed: > 6.5 m/sec
Min avg annual wind speed: 15 km/hr
India total wind potential: ~11,000 MW
TN potential: 6000 MW
Gujarat potential: 5000 MW
India harnessed: 2000 MW

Gujarat wind farm locations: Mandvi-Kutch and Lambha near Dwarka. State with maximum wind energy installed capacity = Tamil Nadu.

Wind Mill / Wind Turbine

A machine that converts kinetic energy of wind into mechanical energy of shaft = wind mill. When connected to generator = wind turbine generator / aerogenerator.

Types of Wind Turbines

HAWT (Horizontal Axis Wind Turbine)

  • Generator placed in the nacelle (nessel) — above the tower
  • Large rotor rotates by lift force
  • Blade design = aerodynamic
  • Generator type = Synchronous induction generator
  • Yaw control = rotates rotor in direction of wind

Savonious Wind Turbine

  • Rotor axis is vertical
  • Used for water pumping / low-power applications
  • Nessel is used in Horizontal Axis Wind Turbine (HAWT)
Applications of Wind Energy / Wind Mills

Applications

  • Generate electricity (wind mills)
  • Run water pumps for irrigation
  • Run flour mills (grinding grains)
  • Run machines without electric power
  • Water pumping and irrigation
  • Electro-metallurgical processes
  • Stand-by energy source
  • Run farm machinery
  • Diamond cutting-polishing factory
  • Domestic lighting
  • Hybrid power plants (with solar)
  • Fishing and salt industries
  • Battery charging
  • Telecommunication in remote areas
  • Rural forestry projects
  • Cottage industries

Wind Farm Converts

Wind energy → Electrical energy

✅ Advantages

  • Completely non-polluting
  • Renewable source
  • No raw material required
  • Initial investment moderate
  • Easy and quick construction
  • Low operation and maintenance costs
  • Cheaper generation
  • No greenhouse gases produced
  • No toxic/radioactive waste
  • Land below turbines can grow crops
  • Highly decentralised installation

❌ Limitations

  • Location must have high wind speed (>6.5 m/s)
  • Wind direction continuously changes
  • Motion is variable and unsteady
  • Power production not uniform → intermittent
  • Noise unsuitable for populated areas
  • Only in vast open areas
  • High wind during thunderstorm may damage system
☀️ Solar Energy

Energy from the sun. The most abundant and continuously available renewable energy source.

Solar constant: 1367 W/m²·μm
N-type material produces free electrons for solar rays

Solar Cell / Photovoltaic

N-type semiconductor material produces free electrons when exposed to solar rays. A strip made by connecting two metals is called a thermocouple.

Silicon material: can resist very high temperature, has high boiling point, is very hard.

🌾 Biomass Energy

Biomass = all types of biological substances: plant products (wood, crop, algae, aquatic plants), their residues (straw, husk, sawdust, cow dung, animal droppings), and waste materials (garbage, night soil).

3 Categories of Biomass Resources

1. Traditional Solid Mass

  • Wood
  • Agricultural residue

2. Non-traditional (Liquid)

  • Biomass converted to liquid fuels

3. Fermented Gas

  • Biogas (methane) from fermentation of biomass

Burning 1 kg of wood → 4000–5000 kcal of heat. Burning biomass in presence of air = bioenergy. Residue = manure for agriculture.

Gas with maximum content in biogas = Methane (CH₄). Biogas digestion takes place in absence of Oxygen. Normally Molasses is used to manufacture ethanol.

Biomass Characteristics
  • Huge quantity and renewable
  • Does not produce environmental pollution
  • Gives fuels of high calorific value
  • Available everywhere
  • Very cheap
  • Can be burned → liquid or gaseous fuel
  • Biomass is a carbonic material

✅ Advantages of Biomass Energy

  • Perennial (plants grow continuously)
  • No pollution, no global warming contribution
  • Very cheap, available everywhere
  • Good potential for rural areas
  • Simple technology, unskilled persons can use
  • Methane used as domestic fuel and to run engines
  • Can be grown in nearby seas/lakes (saves land)
  • Gives good quality manures (byproducts as fertiliser)

❌ Disadvantages of Biomass Energy

  • Contains 50–90% water → heavy, difficult to transport
  • Direct combustion produces smoke and smell
  • Very low calorific value if burnt in raw form
  • Biogas plants need large land areas
  • Biogas plants need lots of care and maintenance
  • Cannot be used in urban areas (limited space)
  • Biogas plants may have odour and insect problems
Uses of Biomass Energy
  • Waste organic biomass directly used as domestic fuel
  • Biogas used for heating water
  • Biogas used as domestic fuel in gas stoves (like LPG)
  • Biogas to run engines, boilers, turbines
  • Biogas (methane) to run farm machinery

Pyrolysis = process using dry biomass. Pyrolysis process provides fuel in all forms (solid, liquid, gas). Biomass is a renewable energy source.

🌋 Geothermal Energy

Heat energy from inside the Earth. As depth increases, temperature increases: for every 125 m depth, temperature rises by 1°C. Interior temperature: 600°C to 6000°C.

Rain water penetrates deep → heated by Earth → becomes vapour → comes to surface as hot water springs.

Gujarat hot water springs: Tulsishyam, Lasundra, Tuva
Himachal Pradesh: 5 kW plant at Manikarnam
Ladakh: thermal wells → salt, sulphur, borex
4 Ways Geothermal Energy is Used
1. Hot water method
2. Wet vapour method
3. Dry vapour method
4. Hot rock method
Uses of Geothermal Energy
  • To generate electric power
  • For greenhouses
  • For fisheries
  • In paper industry

✅ Advantages

  • Renewable, available 24/7
  • No fuel required
  • Low operational cost
  • Small land footprint

❌ Limitations

  • Hot water contains sulphur, ammonia, salts → corrosion of pipes
  • Vapour from earth produces sharp noise when released
  • Difficult to obtain sufficient pressure from such vapour
🌊 Tidal Energy

Periodic rise and fall of sea water = tide. Caused by gravitational forces of sun and moon on Earth.

During high tide → water fills tidal basin → turbine gates open when low tide outside → water flows through turbines → electricity generated.

France tidal plant: 230 MW
India (IIT Chennai): 150 MW in Kerala
India sites: Gulf of Kutch, Cambay, Sundarbans
Minimum head required: 5 m

✅ Advantages

  • Power generation is cheaper (no fuel needed)
  • Does not produce harmful waste
  • Pollution-free
  • Power by natural process of tides
  • No raw material required
  • Does not occupy costly land
  • Site can be used for fishing
  • Tidal power is inexhaustible

❌ Disadvantages

  • Power generation not continuous (tide cycles)
  • If insufficient water head → stop generation
  • Only in coastal areas
  • Very low efficiency
  • High capital investment
  • Operational problems during cyclones/storms
  • Costly transmission
  • Heavy silting → frequent dredging needed
💧 Hydrogen Energy

Hydrogen is a clean fuel and the most abundant element in the universe (90% of all atoms). The sun and stars are composed largely of hydrogen. Water is the most abundant compound of hydrogen on Earth.

Hydrogen is considered an alternative fuel due to its ability to power fuel cells in zero-emission electric vehicles. A fuel cell coupled with electric motor is 2–3 times more efficient than internal combustion engine on gasoline.

Fuel Cell

An electrochemical conversion device that converts hydrogen + oxygen → water, producing electricity and heat. Works like a battery but doesn't need recharging. Hydrogen burns in fuel cell → electricity.

Energy in 1 kg of hydrogen ≈ energy in 2.8 kg of gasoline. Hydrogen fuel cell works like a battery, does not require recharging, produces no harmful emissions.

✅ Advantages

  • Readily available
  • No harmful emissions
  • Environmentally friendly
  • Renewable
  • Lighter and smaller unit
  • Can be used as fuel in rockets
  • Higher fuel efficiency

❌ Disadvantages

  • High initial cost
  • Low voltage
  • Low service life
  • Difficult to store
  • Highly flammable
Potential Applications of Hydrogen
  • Production of electricity, heat and water
  • Industrial applications
  • Vehicular transportation
  • Residential applications
  • Commercial applications (telecom towers backup power)
🏞️ Hydro Energy

Energy generated by the flow of water. Includes large dams (hydroelectric power plants) and small-scale run-of-the-river plants. Clean, renewable, and reliable. Used widely in India (e.g., Bhakra Nangal, Tehri Dam).

🎯 Chapter 4 — Exam Tips (Renewable Energy)
🗣 Gujlish Revision: Renewable vs Non-renewable ane dareek type na advantages/disadvantages — MCQ common aave chhe!
1
Renewable = inexhaustible (sun, wind, water, biomass, geothermal). Non-renewable = limited, will run out (coal, petroleum, natural gas, nuclear). Remember: Renewable ≠ non-polluting (biomass burns!).
2
Solar energy: Photovoltaic (PV) cells convert sunlight to electricity. India has huge solar potential (Rajasthan). Solar cooker uses reflectors to focus sunlight.
3
Wind energy: Wind turbines need min. 15 km/h wind. No pollution during operation. Largest wind farm in India = Tamil Nadu. Wind mills used for pumping water since ancient times.
4
Tidal energy: Uses tidal rise and fall. First major tidal plant = La Rance, France. Limited suitable sites globally. Predictable unlike solar/wind.
5
Biogas (Gobar gas): Mainly methane (CH₄). Produced by anaerobic decomposition of organic waste. Cow dung + water → biogas. Slurry (leftover) = excellent fertilizer.
